PREFACE
The Book of Radiance, is the first volume in Intellectual Traditions,
a series aimed to provide scholars and students of Iranian Islamic
heritage with new source materials. Future publications in the series
will include studies in philosophy, mysticism and ‘Irfan, religion,
intellectual history, and literature.
I wish to express my gratitude to Professor S.H. Nasr for his
```
permission to use his edition of the Persian text; George Maschke
```
for his invaluable and generous assistance with inputting and
```
formatting the computer files; Christine Canzanella and Dinah
```
```
Berland for their editorial assistance; and Firouzeh Papan-Matin
```
for typing portions of the Persian text. Special thanks are due my
students at UCLA for their engaging class discussions during 1998
that helped bring to light a number of philosophical problems in
Partow Nameh. Lastly, I wish to acknowledge the advisory board of
Intellectual Traditions for their guidance and support.
This volume was set in Minion/Bayan-Mashhad on Nisus Writer™,
and I am grateful to the staff of Nisus Software Inc. for their technical
support.

Shihabal-DinYahyaibnHabashibnAmirakAbu’l-FutahSohravardi
wasborninSohravardinnorthwesternIranin 549/1154.Oneofthe
bestknownfigures inthehistory of Islamicphilosophy,his status
asfounderofthephilosophyof illumination earnedhimthehonorific
```

majortheoreticalworkinPersian.7
Sohravardi’s philosophical oeuvre was composed within a span
of ten years. The major works in the corpus are cross-referenced,
which indicates that they were either composed concurrently, or
revised in respect to one another as he taught them.’ Written in
' The major biographical sources on Sohravardi are: Ibn Abi Usaybi‘a, ‘Uyan

Arabic,hismostimportantandnovelwork,ThePhilosophyof

Sohravardi’s biography is fairly well known and has been discussed
in earlier publications.° Some of the more significant events may be
summarized here. Sohravardi entered the service of Prince al-Malik
al-Zahir Ghazi, governor of Aleppo and son of the Ayyubid Salah
al-Din, commonly known as Saladin. The young philosopher’s
special position at court’ was resented by the judges, viziers, and
jurists in Aleppo—especially of the Nuriyya madrasa*— and resulted
in letters from Qadi al-Fadil to Saladin requesting Sohravardi’s
execution.’ According tomedieval historians, Sohravardi was charged

TheBook of RadiancewascomposedfortheSeljukrulerSulayman
Shah,whoissaidtohavebeenagreatpatronofphilosophy.'*This
```

```
ofRadiance,whichwascomposed bycommandofone ofthe‘lovers
ofvirtue.”Theworkisin anelegantphilosophicalPersianin ten
chaptersiinwhichtopicsselectedfromlogic,physics,andmetaphysics
are presented, usually in a straightforward style.
The last chapter closely resembles the final chapters in each of
Sohravardi’s four major works in Arabic where, expounding on
the principles of “Illuminationist political doctrine,” just rule is
associated with learning philosophy and practicing wisdom, as well
as with clairvoyance, true dreams, divine inspiration, and other
such extraordinary qualities.’” It also stipulates the ancient Persian
’ The “syllabus” on illuminationist philosophy begins with a “first” level dis-
cussion of problems in the Intimations in a style and language that is both intro-
ductory and standard. While the text does parallel Avicenna’s Peripatetic texts, it
is not confined to Peripatetic principles and arguments, but goes beyond to
introduce novel illuminationist principles, laws, and arguments. The “final” level
of the syllabus includes a complete presentation of illuminationist philosophy in
both language and method in the text Philosophy of Illumination. I have presented
the details of such characteristics of Sohravardi’s major texts in my, Knowledge
```
and Illumination (Atlanta, 1990), Ch.J; and in “Shihab al-Din Suhrawardi: founder
```
of the Illuminationist school,” in History of Islamic Philosophy, ed S.H. Nasr and
O. Leaman (London: Routledge, 1996), pp. 434-464.

hisZindigani-yiMawlanaJalal al-Din (Tehran, 1987), pp. 24-28.

111
A detailed discussion of illuminationist philosophy is beyond the
scope of this introduction. However, in order to facilitate the reader’s
comprehension of the new illuminationist arguments found in The
Book of Radiance, a brief account of selected principles that serve to
distinguish Suhravardi’s approach from that of Avicennan philos-
ophy iis provided below.
In general, in his philosophy of illumination, Sohravardi deals
```
with a number of problems in the Islamic Peripatetic style; but
```
elsewhere, his arguments follow an illuminationist approach. Some
of these problems are illuminationist by common association, such
```
as the multiplicity of. Intellects (kathrat-e ۷۵: §54-§61); the
```
```
attribution “rich” and “poor” to equivocal being; and his adding of
```
“nobility”and“degree of intensity” to standard Aristoteliantypes
```
ofpriority($50).Other problemsaremore complexandrequirea
```
closerexaminationbelow.
۱۶]have discussedthisaspect of thetext in detail inmy“The Source and
Nature of Authority:A Studyof al-Suhrawardi’s IluminationistPoliticalDoc-
trine,” pp. 230-334.

Suhrawardi’s IlluminationistPoliticalDoctrine,” pp. 317ff

In thedomainofepistemology, Sohravardi’sunifiedtheoryof
```
apprehension(Arabic,idrak,Persian,daryaftan),iswell-knownin
```
```
Islamicphilosophyas“knowledgebyppresence”(“ilm-ehuzuri).Thiseresمیت
```
theoryrestsonaninquiryintotherelationbetweenbeinganand
knowing,whichisfirstrenderedintoPersianinThe BookofRRadiance.
```
“Hereself-consciousnessplays¢afundamentalrole.‘و(mani,
```
```
inPersian)isexpandedtoinclude“thou-ness”(to’z)and“he/she/it-
```
```
ness”(i‘i);i.e.ipseity(huwiyya)isgeneralizedandencompasses
```
```
thesecond andthirdpersonsaswell(§27,$44).Thesubject,orthe
```
```
“one who apprehends/knows” (Arabic, mudrik; Persian, daryaban-
```
```
, deh) apprehends the object (mudrak; darydafteh) when an atemporal
```
جاسک relation is actualized between them. Self-consciousness/self-
apprehension does not derive from the dyadic differentiation of
being but ispriorto any differentiation. Thus, “I think” and "1 am”
are “sameness,” which is a non-predicative identity relation. In The
Book of Radiance, this relation is named be-ham bidan, lit.,
```
‘togetherness’ ofsubject (daryabandeh) and object (daryafteh); and
```
```
isapplied to the rational soul (nafs-e nateqeh) and body (tan; 532,
```
```
57(
```
Togetherness is an identity preserving one-to-one correspondence
between each and every member of the set of all knowing subjects
and knowable objects. Sohravardi here extends a logical principle
of identity on to a metaphysical principle of relational correspon-
dence. Each side of the relation is defined as a continuous domain
consisting of multiple things, and the sum total of all things
constitutes the whole. Every member of each of the two domains is
```
said to be self-consciouss, which is further attributed with “living”;
```
therefore, the whole is also said to be self-conscious and thus “alive”
```
(hayy; §41-46).
```
The reasons for Sohravardi’s departure from Avicenna’s Aristote-

Introduction
lianmethodology—wherethefirstprinciplesareconstructedaspred-
icative,essentialistdefinitions—are complex. Toputitsuccinctly,
hequestionstheuniversalvalidityofpredicativelaws.Aristotelian
scientifictheorymustnecessarilybeconstructedonprinciplest!that
aremostpriorandmostwellknown.Aristotlereiteratesthatsuch
principlescannotbeobtainedbysyllogisticdemonstration.And
thoughhestatesinmorethanoneplace, thatprinciples ofscience

```

```
```
“immediate |knowledge”) he iis ‘uncertain regarding theirvalidity,
```
never onsale distinguishing ‘‘immediate knowledge”. from
“opinion. > This was regarded as ambiguous by later philosophers,
andSohravardi tried to overcome the problem. For example,
Aristotle names the necessary primary knowledge of first principles
a kind of “immediate knowledge,” or “intuition” and states that it
is “a starting-point of knowledge,” but then leaves it unclear as to
whether this type of pre-inference knowledge is “opinion,” valid by
“common acceptance,” or something known as “scientific knowl-
edge.” This ambiguity is resolved by Sohravardi’s
```
(‘lm al-anwar), which is the methodology of his new philosophy of
```
<Science of Lights”
illumination.

universal aaffirmative, al-mujiba al-kulliya; i.e., Vaf(a)) are both

necessary and always true, or universal. Through an elaborate process

texts, Sohravardi establishes future contingency (al-imkan_ al-soos NEtzeSeieoeenrenne

IV
Partow Nameh has been printed twice before: first, by Seyyed

here named S; and second, by Seyyed Hossein Nasr in Opera

```
Metaphysica et Mystica III (1970),”’ here named N. The present edition
```
is based on S, N, and one other manuscript, F, which was identified
and described by Helmut Ritter in 1937-38.” F is one of the oldest
```
known manuscripts (mss #5426; copied in 726 A.H.), and is housed
```
in the Soltan Fatih Library in Istanbul. The text of the present
edition does not differ greatly from N, which, however, shows major
differences with S. In preparing it for publication, I have compared
all three texts and added punctuation and voweling where necessary
to facilitate reading. In the Persian text whenever «Sand « are used
as logical connectives in compound syllogisms, I have added a
“comma” to distinguish from their use as pronouns.

In the Name of God, Whose Assistance Ensures Success
```
{1] Know that this is a short treatise called The Book of Radiance,
```
which was composed in compliance with the command of one of
the “loversof virtue.” In certain instances where the terminology of
Peripatetic philosophers was difficult to understand, we have
elaborated on the terms for clarification. The aim is to expound on
some issues in the science of metaphysics, some of which we have
presented in the past. Other scholars and the Giver of Life are
implored to facilitate the journey to its successful end. This work
comprises ten chapters.

- CHAPTER ONE “
```
On the Explanation of Some Philosophical Terms; and on سس تتجنس HALA ۱
```
Defining the Body and Some of Its States
[2] Know that whatever you perceive, your cognizance and knowledge
of it is such that aform of thatthing isobtained by you, because
you have come to know something you previously did not know.
For if something of the thing’s image were not obtained by you,
then your state before and after knowledge would be the same,
which is impossible. Furthermore, if that which you have obtained
does not completely correspond with the thing you have come to
Inow, then you have not acquired knowledge of it, as-Ht-is.Therefore,
it is necessary that what you acquire corresponds to the thing as-it-is
in itself and that this be its true form.
[3] Know that whenever the meaning of a single “term” applies
equally to many things, we call it a “general term,” and we call its
meaning a “universal meaning.” And whenever the meaning of a
ear term is associated with only one ue we call it a “Patiieulat
ae humans partake, even as the term “human” applies equally
to Zayd, ‘Amr, and Khalid. Examples of the particular term are
Zayd, Bakr, “this man,” and whatever is specifically indicated.

attributionis either: “) necessary, such as the predicate “animal”

```

because of the fire, you will then deduce that every fire that exists
has the attribute of “burning.” Any attribute predicated as a proven
deduction for the thing itself, however, does not necessarily

5 The Book of Radiance
anecessaryattributefor“body-ness,” otherwiseitwouldalsobe
necessaryforwatertoburn. Rather,“burning”is anecessaryattribute

[6] Know that “induction” is a conclusion based on examining
numerous particulars. An example of this is when someone says:
“All animals move the lowerjjaw when they chew, because in whatever
animals we have observed, from cows and horses to humans and
other animals, we have found this to be true.” This method of
reasoning is incorrect, however, because thepossibility exists that
the person has not yet observed the evidence contrary to [the
conclusion drawn from] what he did observe. For example, the
crocodile moves its upper jaw when it chews. Hence, in this case,
the induction “all animals move the lower jaw when they chew” is
proven to be false. [In sum,] if someone does not actually observe
something, or does not know of it, this does not prove the
nonexistence of that thing. True philosophers, when drawing a
general conclusion, ponder the principle of a thing, and based on
careful analysis, they then deduce the necessity, possibility, or
impossibility of a thing’s attributes. Thus, the proposition “All
humans are animals” is not constructed by means of induction.
Rather, [it is deduced, because] the attribute “animal” is necessary
to conceive the essence of “human” in the mind, whether for one
human, or for many.
[7]Knowthat, in thisshortepistle,wewill use theterm“attribute”
to refer toany qualitywhoseexistenceisina realthing suchthatit
isaltoogether subsumed bythat thing,suchas“blackness”or

```
“that which does have a substratum.” These are easily grasped
definitions. But [to explain comprehensively] the way the Peripatetic
philosophers employ ,the technical terms “substance” and
“accident”—and to distinguish among their uses of “accident,”
“attribute,” and “form”—would unduly protract this discussion.
Prior to the Peripatetics, these technical terms were defined and
```
known as we present them here; our intention is primarily to indicate
```
the “meaning” [signified by the term]. We will call every substance
```
that by necessity is extended in length, width, and depth “body”;
```
and we shall define body as a substance that can be physically
indicated [i.e., “this thing,” “that thing,” etc.].

differenceamongtheparticularsmustbesomethingother thanthe
sharedquality [byvirtueofwhichtheyareincludedinthe universal].
Sinceallbodiesareincludedintheuniversal“body-ness,”there
```
mustbeadifferencebetweenparticular bodies;andthisdifference
```
isexpressedbyattributes.puesdifferfromeach othereither
in terms of: 4 the “what-ness” ofa thing, as when something is
called “black” or “white” [by virtue of which it is distinguished
```
from another]; or ‘2)in terms of the “where-ness” of a thing, such
```
as when two things are called “black” but differ because each is
situated in a different substratum [i.e., “this thing is here” and
“that thing is there”]. Should it happen that both black bodies
occupy the “same” substratum, then they must differ in terms of
time. One will be in that substratum at one time and the other at a
different time. Should it happen that both bodies exist at the same
time and in the same substratum, then they would be one and the
same, which is impossible. From this we deduce that two like things
cannot exist in the same substratum at the same time.
[9]Knowthatanattributecannot move fromonesubstratumto

another;forifitcouldmove byitself,itwouldbecomeextendedin

directioninthe future,and whateverhas thesixdirectionsmust

(10)Knowthatsomepeople[thedialecticaltheologians]have put
```
forwardthepropositionthateverybodycanbedividedtothe point
thatitsfurtherdivisibilitycannotbeconceivedinthemind.They
favecalledthis[ultimate smallestatom]the“singularsubstance.”
Butthe philosophersholdthatitisimpossibleforabodytohavea

faculties,suchasestimation.Ofcourse,itmaybetrue that inthe
externalworld,duetotheexistenceofanactualinfinitesimal thing,

and touchingtwoothersubstances[S,andS,].Ifیرblocks S,from

parts and thatit isdivisible infinitely in the mind. Our opponents

9 The Book of Radiance
object that: If a pebble and a mountain are both infinitely divisible,
then they would be equal to each other. This [objection] is not
worthy of attention, because we are not saying that this infinite
divisibility takes place in the external world—rather it is in the
mind, where we can consider one division after another.
It isalso possible that two thingsbe infiniteinthe mind, and that
onebe larger than the other. For example, compare two sequences:
all possible multiples of 10 [c,] to all possible multiples of 100 [c,].
The number of possible multiples of 10 is greater than the number
of possible multiples of 100, because in every 100 there are ten 10s,
although in both [sequences], numbers can be increased to infinity.

ortennumbersfromasequence.Thenonecouldjoinbothendsof

additionalamountwill alsomatchthedecreasedamount, whichis

andtheaddedpartwouldnot.Thus,thedecreasedpartwillbe
finite,theaddedpartwilladd onlyafiniteamounttothesequence
orthe extension, and whateverisaddedtoafiniteamountwill also
befinite.

12}Knowthat sincethebodyisnecessarilyfinite,itmustalso
```
haveaspecifiedquantityandaspecifiedshape.Ifthisquantityand
shapewererequiredofabody—quaonlybody—itwouldthen
```
followthatallbodieswouldbethesameinquantityandshape;
```
whereasthisisnotthecase.Youhavealsolearnedthatbodies
```

```
ceasedtoexist,thenthemultiplicity ofbodiesandtheirexistence
wouldbe impossible.Furthermore,ifthese attributes,bywhich
bodiesaredistinguished, wererequiredof“body-ness,” thenall
bodieswouldbe equalinrespecttothem.Sincethe finitudeof
bodieshasbeenestablished,thenwhatgivesquantity,attributes,
andexistencetobodiesis“something”thatis neitheritselfa body
nor a bodily attribute.
[13] Know that people point to directions, such as “above” and
“below.” Movements differ in relation to whether they are from
above or from below. If directions were not something real, then
people could not point to them, for moving toward or pointing
toward nothing is impossible. Furthermore, if all directions appeared
the same to an observer, then there would be no real distinction
among them, so the priority of directions in terms of “above-ness”
and “below-ness” would not exist. Because bodies are finite in every
dimension, this implies that the possible reference points of a real
thing are also limited and this limit is not divisible. Otherwise,
there would be no real things beyond the posited upper bound,
and the motion associated with bodies would be unreal and directed
toward something nonexistent, which is an impossibility. Therefore,
that which is the all-encompassing limit of all directions, beyond
which no real thing can exist, cannot itself be divisible. It is also not

conceivable, thenthat“void”inwhichabodycouldfitwouldbe
greaterthanthatwhichislesser—namely,thebodycontainedinit.
Thus,“more”and“less”wouldbeinthe void,andhalf,inclination,

(15] Know that the quantity of specific bodies is something added
```
to the idea of “body,” because all bodies share alike in the universal
[general term] “body-ness,” while they differ in respect to quantity.
Therefore, quantity is an accident in “body.” If “void” is postulated
as a quantity not in a body, then it must be—by virtue of
```
itsel{—independent of a substratum, which would imply that all
```
quantities are independent of substratum, which is clearly not the
case. Therefore, “void” is impossible, and every part of the universe
is copious. The place of a body is where it is situated, and no other
body can coexist with it in the same place, but a body may move
from its situated place. From this, we conclude that “substance” is
not the place of attributes, because attributes cannot change position
[if something is to remain what it is]. Also, “where” a body is
situated is not a real [or fixed] place, because one cannot say that
the body is in it [for it may move away from it]. Therefore, the
place of a body is the inner limit of the all-encompassing body,
which is contiguous with the outer limit of the encompassed body.
A body cannot be in the same body at the same time, and a body
```
cannot be in a different body at the same time; for this would
```
imply the inclusion [of many bodies] in one, all touching the limit.

16]Knowthateveryaccidentthatcannotbeconceivedtobe
stationarymustbe“motion.”Also,knowthat foranythingthat
wasnonexistent butcameinto existence,itsnonexistenceisprior
toitsexistence.This“priority”isthesameasthe [former]
nonexistence of something1ggenerated iin time, such that ‘only afterarene
ithas become existent will it continue to exist. Priority qua priority
is never combined with posteriority. This priority is not itself a real
thing that exists together with a generated thing in contrast to a
cause, a substance, or an accident—inasmuch as the latter three
can all exist along with something generated. As the priority of the
thing generated does not exist together with it, it is thus a non-static
accident, which is “motion.” There are “priors” more “prior” than
others, and there are differences among them. Therefore, they have
quantity and are thus priorities in terms of time. “Time” is the
“measure of motion,” as when we think of “before” and “after”
[some event] in the mind. But it is not the measure of any type of
```
motion; rather, time is the measure of continuous motion—and
```
the most apparent of all. Such motion, for example, is the daily
movement of the sun from the east to the west. Hence, if time were
something [itself] altogether generated in time—in such a way as
to have a “beginning” and its “place’—then it would also need to
have a “before,” which is also “time,” as we have already explained.
Therefore, before any particular measurement of time, an [eternal]
time—as well as an [eternal] motion—would need to exist from
which all variable time, place, and motion are derived. However,
should there be a specifiable “interruption” of this primary time
and its substratum, then it also must have an “after.” In this case,
```
the argument would lead in two ways: ‘)) there would be a specific
```

15 The Book of Radiance
timebeforeand_afteralltime,whichisabsurd—if timewerwere
associated with linear motion, then it could not continue indefinitely,
```
and its end would be knowable; or (2) if time were to return after
```
an interval, still the same would apply—for this would necessitaterene
```
that time be‘‘interrupted” [i.e., be discrete, not continuous; and, as
```
we have said, this is impossible. Therefore, time is the measure of
“circular motion,” which has no limit. Any motion that requires a
potentiality associated with body, when it can only move in a single
direction, is called “natural”—such as the motion of a stone falling
toward a given point. Should the motion propelling a body be
capable of moving in many directions, it is called “voluntary.” But
if something is moved by something else, its motion is called
“compulsory” constraint—such as the movement of a stone upward
[by external force]. Every body has a natural rest place to which,
when moved away from by constraint, it desires to aa
Furthermore, each kind of body has a specific place appropriate to
it not associated with body-ness in general to which, if not
constrained, it would incline. The all-encompassinglimit has no
“place,” because beyond it there is no body.

16TheBookofRadiance
thatishomogeneousinallitspartsisthesphere.Boththeall-
encompassinglimitandthecircumferencearesimple.Therefore,
theymustbespherical.Itisalsoimpossiblethattwodifferent
universesbesubsumedundertwoall-encompassinglimits.Thisis
becauseonelimitwouldthenrequireadirection differentfrom the
other,whichwouldimplythatyetanother direction existsothother
thanthetwosupposed,withanall-encompassinglimit ofitsown.
Therefore,thesuppositionoftwoall-encompassinglimitsdoesnot
hold.Furthermore,twosphericalshapeswithoutan intervalbetween
themcannotcometogether.Thiswouldimplyavoidbetweenthem,
and “void” is impossible [as we have shown].
[18] It is also not possible that a single species of body have two
```
[specific] places; for if such were the case, the body would require
```
neither one, both spaces being equal to it. Thus it would not move
toward either of them. But bodies naturally move from one direction
toward another. From this, we deduce that bodies have a single
“restplace.” Composite bodies tend to move toward the dominant
place. Note that a perfectly balanced composite of the four elements
is not possible, because there is no common boundary between
each of the four equidistant from [the restplace of the body] so that
the body could come to rest there and not incline toward one of
the four elements [more than another]. If this were so, it would not
need any restplace, which is absurd.

from among the simple things which is like fire, ranks above all
other elemental entities beneath the sphere of the moon. It is also

It islight becauseof its nearness to fire. The third is cold and

inclines toward the lowermost limit. Water is heavy because of its
nearness to earth. That earth is heavier than water is evident, for
whenever we pour some earth into water, it sinks, while the water
remains standing over the earth. As for air being lighter than water,
this is proved whenever you submerge a leathern bottle full of air
into water: it will undoubtedly surface. Some people have thought
that water compels the bottle to rise, because whenever water comes
together [where the bottle is resting], it exerts force on it and pushes
it up. But this is a false supposition, because [we know that] heavier
bodies have greater resistance to external force. It would then have

[20] Know that the ethereal bebodies, by which we mean the celestial
bodies, are neither heavy nor light, because lightness 1isa potential
```
in bodies to move from the center upward; while heaviness Sis a
```
potential in bodies to move downward toward the middle. The
celestial bodies move neither toward a center nor away from a
center, but rather they move around a center in a circular motion,
never making or accepting any form of linear motion. Therefore,
the celestial spheres cannot be characterized as hot, cold, humid, or
dry.
```
(21) Know that the four elements can change into one another.
```
```
Water, given intense heat, will turn into air; and air subjected to
```
cold will turn into water. For example, if a dry vessel is filled with
ice and covered, drops of water will appear on its outer surface.
These drops of water did not seep out from inside the vessel, because
not even hot water seeps out of a vessel, let alone ice water. Therefore,
the drops were [particles of] air that became cold due to their
proximity to the vessel and turned into water. Water can also turn
```
into earth; since [it is observed that] in certain places, when water
```
drips it turns into stone. Conversely, earth can turn into water.
Magicians can turn a stone into water by their acts, and air blown
with vigormayturnintofire.Aflame offire instantlyturnsinto

19 The Book of Radiance
air,forwereittoremainas fire,itwouldburntheceilingorwhatever
else is above it, while this is not the case.
[22] Know that when we say “water turns into air,” this does not
mean that water in all its parts totally ceases to exist and turns into
air, because then water could not become air. Nor is it that water,
while it is water, also becomes air, because a single thing cannot be
both water and air at the same time. Rather, the thing which had
the form of water becomes transformed into air as the form of
water leaves it. The thing which accepts all these different forms is
called “Prime Matter.” Prime Matter extended is called “body.” In
the form of water it is called “water,” and in the form of earth it is
called “earth.” The Prime Matter of all beings composed of the
four elements is the same. When one form leaves Prime Matter,
another takes its place. But the “ethereal” things are not subject to
generation and corruption, because their Prime Matter is not the
same.
[23] Know that the form “water-ness” is not exactly the same as
```
“humidity” and “coldness”; because when we heat water, its coldness
```
```
disappears; and when it turns into ice, its humidity disappears—all
```
this while it is still water. Therefore, the forms “water-ness,” “fire-
» 6ness,” “air-ness,” and “earth-ness” are different. fromthe Four
Natures—hot,cold,humid,and dry—and[wemaynotethat]there
are imperceptiblethings thatmaycometo resideinPrimeMatter

corruption”occurswhenonefform[associatedwithathing]leaves
itand anotherform‘takesitsplace.Corruptionisassociatedwith
whathas ceased,and generationisassociatedwiththatwhichhas
appeared.Somepeoplehaverejectedthepossibility of

notduetothewaterbecominghot butisduetothemixingofthe

However, [we observe] that this is not the case.
[24] Furthermore, heat is caused by intense motion and the friction
of bodies rubbing together, without particles of fire passing between
them. The same people, however, have said that heat and particles
of fire are concealed inside bodies, and when bodies are moved
they come out [producing heat]. This position is also false, because
we observe that water and other liquids are altogether cold, both
internally and externally. If we agitate them severely and continually,
we observe that the liquid becomes uniformly warm. If [the position
held by our adversaries were true], then only the outer part of the
liquid should have become warm, while the interior should have
remained cold, and this is not the case. As you know, heat is merely

[25] Whatever arises from the earth—namely, things that are easily
diffused and the like—is rarefied by heat. Among them, whatever is
dry and not black is called “smoke.” Whatever is humid is called
“vapor.” When vapor ascends and becomes cold, it becomes massive
```
and turns into clouds; and if the cold becomes more intense, the
```
vapor will turn into rain and pour down. In baths, people observe
vapor rising and becoming massive, such that it heats up the vessels
in the bath, covering them with moisture, which in turn drips on
the floor. When vapor encounters more intense cold, it solidifies

- CHAPTER THREE
An Inquiry into the Self
[27]Knowthatalthough youmay[atsometime]ceasetofeelany
orevery partofyour body, andsomebodilypartsmayevenbecome
annihilated,yetahumanbeing’slifeandperceptiondonotdecline
onaccountofthis.Somepartsofthebody—suchasthebrain,the
comparingthemtootherparts.Inshort,youmaybecut offfrom
any bodilyorcontingent perceptionbutwillremaincognizantof
yourselfandknow“yourself”without recoursetoanyphenomenal
thing.Therefore,your“essentialself”or“thou-ness”isnot
dependentonthebody,eitherinpartorinwhole.Ifitwereinside
apartofyour body,thenyoucouldnever beconsciousofyour
“thou-ness” withoutthat part.Therefore,youressentialselfor“T-
ness” is not limited to the bodily nor accidental.
[28] Another inquiry is this: You call yourself “I,” but you refer to
all the parts of your body, each one of them, as “it.” And whatever
you call “it” cannot be the speaker of the “I” in you, because that
which you regard as “it” cannot be your “I” at the same time. Were
it so, you would have to separate it from itself and from its own
“I-ness” and this is impossible. Thus, all things pertaining to you
called “it” are neither all of you nor part of you. If it were possible
for you to separate a part of yourself from your “I-ness,” your
“I-ness” would not remain as “I-ness.” Just as if you separate even
one part from a house, like a wall or a door, it can no longer be
calledahouse.Therefore, since youreferto all your bodily

[29] Another proof regarding the self is this: You know the idea of
“animal” as a universal such that you are able to predicate it ofan
elephant just as you predicate it of a mosquito. You have not
determined a specific measure or a specific nature for this general
animal. Thus, there exists a form in you that can be predicated
equally to all [particular animals] differentiated by measure and
place. If this general meaning “animal” in your mind required taking
on the particularized form of an elephant, then it is certain that it
would no longer be general and could no longer be predicated in
the same manner to a mosquito. But since the idea “animal” is
predicated equally of all [animal] kinds—and it has no specific
shape, measure, or position—then it does enter into a body or
```
anything subsisting in a body; for bodies have quantifiable attributes.
```
Whatever is determined by a body will necessarilyresult in a specific
position and shape. Therefore, the general form cannot be something
distinguishable by means of measurement, position, and shape.
Moreover, since it is predicated of many things simultaneously,
```
and this form is in you; your essential self is, therefore, neither a
```
body nor anything in a body nor anything extended in space.
[30] Another proof is this: You know that the meaning of “absolute
one” is something that does not accept divisibilityin any way atall.
Now, were the form “one” in a body, then—since bodies are divisible,
and whatever is in a body is also divisible—it follows that the form
“one” would also be divisible and thus could not be the form “one”
[which is a contradiction]. Therefore, that in you in which the

“+ CHAPTER Four
On the Faculties of the Soul
[33]Knowthat,since thehumanconstitutionisnobler thanthe
constitution of otheranimalsandplants,anoblersoulisobtained
in incorporeal humans—a perceptive and intelligent soul that
employs at the same time allthe animal |faculties. In animals, the
vegetative faculties exist in addition to the faculties of sensation
and voluntary motion. The principal vegetative faculties are three:
The first is the faculty of nutrition, a ‘faculty that works on food
that has been consumed by turning it into a nutritive substance,
and then discarding the residue of what is broken up. Without the
nutritive faculty, animals and others species could not exist. The
second faculty is growth, which increases the substance of the
nourished parts [of the body] in proportion to what they already
contain, for acquiring mass is part of growth. If this faculty did not
exist, animals and other species could not grow to perfection. The
third faculty is the reproductive faculty, due to which parts of the
matter of an animal or |plant are s¢separated from it so that another
individual can be reproduced. Because of this faculty, the species of
every plant and animal continues to exist even though itsindividualNeca el netteeee—
members do not.
The faculty of nutrition is itself served by four faculties: The faculty
```
of attraction, which obtains food; the faculty of retention, which
```
```
retains food so an individual can use it; the faculty of digestion,
```
```
which digests food and prepares it for nutritive uses; and the faculty
```
of excretion, which discards waste. The nutritive faculty exists prior
to the reproductive faculty, and the reproductive faculty continues
to function after the faculty of growth has ceased. The nutritive

29 The Book of Radiance
faculty continues to function after the reproductive faculty has
ceased, lasting until death. Animals also have a “motor faculty” and
an “appetitive faculty.” The appetitive faculty has two modes of
```
expression: “anger,” by which one repels what is undesired; and
```
desire, by which one attracts the desired. It is the appetitive faculty
that causes motion in the individual animal through desire.
[34] There are two kinds of senses: “outer” and “inner.” As for theer و همسر
outer senses, these are five: the first is “touch,” which is a faculty
spread over the entire surface of the body. The four natures [hot,
cold, dry, and moist—as well as lightness and heaviness, softness
and hardness, smoothness and roughness—are all perceived by this
sense. The second is “taste,” which is a faculty located in the nerves
distributed on the surface of the tongue. By means of moisture
present in the nerves of the tongue, this faculty perceives tastes, so
that the taste of whatever comes in contact with it is perceived. The
third is “smell,” which is a faculty in the brain’s two frontal
protuberances, which resemble breast nipples. Smells are perceived
by this faculty by means of [inhaled] air, which holds the scent of
things, and by the vapor emitted from their bodies. The fourth is
“hearing,” which is a faculty located in the holes of the ears. By this
faculty, sounds carried in the air are perceived. “Sounds” are
produced by air waves, as when bodies are struck together or when
heavy objects fall. From such occurrences, air is forcefully expelled
from between the bodies and vibrates until it reaches the air in the
earhole,causingittovibrate in similar fashion. This vibration then

۳ همانوترپ
- CHAPTER Five ¢
On the Essence of the Necessary Being and Its Attributes
[37] Know that the Necessary Being is that which cannot possibly
not exist, rather it must necessarily exist. Impossible Being is that
which cannot possibly exist. And Possible Being is that which has
the possibility either to exist or not to exist. The possible becomes
necessary by the condition of the existence of its cause and becomes
impossible by the condition of the nonexistence of cause. Regarding
existence or nonexistence, when one ponders the essence of such a
thing itself, one will find that it is possible. When a possible thing’s
existence depends on, and is caused by, something else, then that
thing is possible in itself. For should it be necessary in and of itself,
then by the nonexistence of something else it would not cease to
exist. Therefore, since a possible thing’s existence is due to something
```
else, it is possible in and of itself; but a possible thing does not exist
```
by itself, because the possibility of existence in itself is not greater
than the possibility of its nonexistence. Rather, the priority for its
existence is due to the existence of its cause, and the priority for its
nonexistence is due to the nonexistence of its cause. Otherwise,
should a possible thing’s existence be due to itself, then it must
necessarily exist and will itself be necessary, while we had postulated
it to be possible.
[38] We name a thing’s “cause” by that which the thing’s existence
is obtained and by which it becomes necessary. Should a possible
existent thing be caused by something’s opposite, such that the
opposite must exist for the thing to exist, then every constituent of
the opposite is part of the cause, and together they comprise the
cause itself. Whenever a cause is obtained by the sum total of the
-34-

the upper limit of the sequence of all causes. We have also proven
that the sequence of causes and effects has an upper limit as something
that has no cause itself, and that thing is the Necessary Being, Exalted
be His Glory.

37 The Book of Radiance
cause. Thus, it would follow that one or the other be possible and
not necessary. But should the necessity of one or the other not be
qua the necessity of existence of that one, then it must again be due
to something else. This would mean that it must be due to a cause,
from which it would also follow that one, or the other, be possible
and not necessary. This is a contradiction, since we postulated that
they both be necessary. Now, should someone argue that “Cif] it is
certain that the Necessary Being exist, it must be one,” but “why
have you claimed that the Necessary Being exists?” To such an
objection we respond thus: Since it has been proven that the
Necessary Being is one, and that bodies and accidents are not one
but many. It is then [proven] that the multiple bodies and accidents
cannot be necessary but are possible. All possible things, [as
previously proven] require[a cause and] also abeing most prior in
respect to them. We thus conclude that the Necessary Being is the
upper limit [of the sequenceof]all causes.
[41] Another proof is this: Bodies are composed of form and Prime
Matter and need specific attributes for their existence, so they are
not necessary beings. Specific attributes of forms exist in bodies, so
they cannot be necessary beings either. In addition, it is not possible
for two things to be the cause of one another. For this would mean
that one of the two exists prior to the other and thus exists prior to
itself and so would cause its own cause, which is absurd. Therefore,
we conclude that since bodies are possible beings, they must
necessarily require the Necessary Being to exist [and since they do
exist, the Necessary Being must also exist].
[42] Another proof is this: You have known that rational souls
cannot exist prior to bodies and are thus temporally generated

```

```

essence of the Necessary Being accepts an effect, neither from

```

it would be composite and multiple, which we have provento be
impossible. Also, action as an act of bestowing perfection is more
noble than passion as an act of receiving, which is a relative
imperfection. The result of all of this would be that the essence of
the Necessary Being be composed of two modes: one noble and
one base. Now should the modes be in the Necessary Being’s essence,
```

```

```

```

[44] Know that when you know yourself, you do so not because
of a form of thou-in--thou, because knowing your thou-ness by a
form can be in only one of two ways: either you know that the
form of your thou-ness is equal to thou or you do not. If you do
not know that the form is the same as your thou-ness, then you
would not know your self, while we are here assuming that you do
know it. If you do know that the form of your thou-ness is equal to
thou, then you would have known yourself with the form of your
thou-ness so as to know that it is equal to your thou. Therefore
your knowledge of yourself is not by the form. It can only be that
your essence is self-subsistent, free from corporeality and always
self-cognizant.' And any thing whose essence is absent from you, |sO
you cannot perceive its essence, then you cannot [also] perceive its
form.’ However, the Necessary Being is an essence free from

- CHAPTER Six “
On the Activity of the Necessary Being
[47] Know that from the one, which is one in reality and unique in
respect to every mode, only one [other thing] maybe obtained. For
if two things are obtained from it, then the exigency of one of them
will not be the same as that of the other. Because if their two
requirements were the same, then they would be exactly the same.
Thus, a mode from which one thing is exigent must differ from the
other’s mode. A multiplicity of modes and attributes is impossible
```
in the Necessary Being; It is one with respect to every mode. Therefore
```
```
only one thing becomes existent due to It; and this thing cannotbe
```
a body, because a body is a composite of form and Prime Matter
and is further necessarily distinguished by specific differences. This
indicates that bodies are multiple, which in turn requires that their
causes be multiple, while the Necessary Being is beyond and absolved
of multiplicity. But attributes and forms require a place in which to
```
exist; therefore, that which is obtained from the Necessary Being is
```
neither a thing extended spatially, nor a body, nor [a] bodily
[attribute]. It is therefore not possible that asoul or body be obtained
from the Necessary Being, and thus that which is obtained from it
-43-

every perspective; whatever is such we call ‘ ‘intellect.”

[48] Know that philosophers have said that it is not possible for
the Necessary Being not to act and then act later in time. Because if
```
It is the most prior Being of all things; then since priority and
```
firstness are eternal, It must be eternal. And if the Necessary Being
is obtained, but nothing possible is obtained, then possibles-qua-
possible have no First dependent on a temporal extension nor a
condition nor a state nor the removal of an obstacle to their being
nor the existence of a will. But prior to the existence of all possible
things, a temporal extension—as a condition—could not have been
determined such that all beings would depend on it. And, there is
no specific state in absolute, unrestricted nonexistence so that it be
given preference by the Necessary Being. It will be said about anything
that you determine to be generated in time—due to a will, a
condition, or a time—that its existence is continualif the First is
SO.
In sum, the essence and attributes of God are eternal, and prior
to all possible beings, nothing else exists except the Necessary Being.
If it is the most prior existence, itmust. have eternal preeminence,
and if existence is not obtained from it, it will never be obtained.
Because before the existence of a possible being, nothing exists
with it to act as a cause. It is a false fancy to say, as some people do,
that “if the First Being and its effects are together eternal, they must
be the same andequal.” Forexample, youcanknowthat“whena

45 The Book of Radiance
finger moves, then the ring will also move,” but you never say,
“The ting moves, therefore the finger. moves,’ because the ring’ 5
movement is caused by the finger’s movement and not the other
way around. Moreover, when the ring’s movement ceases, the finger’s
movement must have ceased prior to it. Therefore the finger’s
movement is prior to the ring’s movement in the mind, even though
it may not be prior in time. Therefore, the cause—-and.not temporal
priority—has essential priority in relation to the effect, even though
their motion may be together and continuous. The same situation
is the priority of “breaking” to “being broken.” Theexistence of an
effectisdue to the cause and not vice versa. 7
[49] Know that the reason for the effect’s attachment to the cause
does not involve a temporal “before,” because the nonexistence of
“before” has no impact on the coming to being of the thing generated
in time due to a cause. It is simply attached to its cause becauseits
being possible is made necessary through the cause. Otherwise, if
its existence would continually depend on it, then it would be a
continual effect. Since a possible being is always so, never becoming
a necessary being by essence, then as long as it exists it will be due
to the continual existence of the cause. For should the cause—which
is prior to the effect—not exist, then the effect would not exist
either. Indeed, it is possible that something be the cause of something
else’s existence and the cause of the permanence of another
thing—for example, the form of a statue whose existence is caused
by the sculptor, while the cause of its withstanding is the dryness of

46 The Book of Radiance
the substance of the statue. It is also possible that the cause of its
existence and permanence be the same—for example, a vessel holding
water, because the water takes its form from it and keeps its form
because of it also. The cause of existence and permanence of all
possible beings is nothing other than the Necessary Being. The
```
existence and permanence of all things iis because of It; in thermind,
```
It is prior to the potentiality of existence, because potentiality itself
is caused by It. And should the possible being exist forever, it does
not have potential existence by itself, but. rather bysomething else.
And the nonpotentiality of existence which it has in itself is prior in
the mind to potentiality of existence, which depends on something
```
else. Its existence comes after because of something else’s essence;
```
for, if a possible being would itself have potential existence in its
own essence, then it would not require something else to exist.
Therefore in every possible being there is a prior nonpotentiality of
existence which is a mental, ideal prioritya1and not a temporal one.
This type of generation ofexistence we name Geen cee
It is possible to say that a possible being has non-potential existence
in itself, but not that it has potential existence in itself—for this
would mean that it be an impossible existence. And here we shall
present a point:
[50] Know that when a lower possible being comes into existence,
a more noble one must have existed before it. This is because, if a
lower possible being is obtained from the mode uniqueness of the
Necessary Being, and if we may suppose the existence of a more

47 The Book of Radiance
noble possible being, then the latter could not have been obtained
from the Necessary Being. This is because the Necessary Being is
one in every way, so should It depend on a less noble being, then
no other being—more noble or not—could have been obtained
from It. This would mean then, that, for the supposed more noble
being, a more noble cause than the Necessary Being must
exist—which is impossible, since neither in the mind nor in outer
reality is something nobler than the Necessary Being. This means,
we conclude, that the more noble possible being must be obtained
prior to the less noble one. Furthermore, since we know bodies,
accidents, and rational souls to exist, and we also know that a
noncorporal substance, free in every way from matter, is more
noble than a substance that is corporeal and not free from matter,
then such a noncorporal substance must exist prior to the existent
substances we mentioned, and it is not impossible for a substance
to exist not in matter. How is this? We demonstrated before that
the soul is not in matter, and it may be a substance free from
matter. Therefore, the first thing obtained from the Necessary Being
is a substance, and this is the “intellect.” And, from this law, it also
follows that existence of the worlds cannot be more noble than
they are.

On the Upper Limits and Order of Being
[51] Know that the “rich” inreality isththat which requires nothing in———
its essence and attributes. Whatever requires something else inOates
essence 0or۲ attributes iis “poor. ” The true “King” is He who possesses
the essence of all things but whose own essence does not belong to
anything else. Thus, Kingdom and absolute‘‘tichness” belong to
the Necessary Being, whom everyone needs in terms of existence
and perfection, while He does not require anything. He is the absolute
magnanimous, and God’s bounty is that He bestoweth whatever is
needed without requiring any recompense. Whoever gives in order
that he be praised۳ or that he not be blamed, or that he
be thanked, is not an absolute munificent, rather he is bartering: he
gives something and takes something back. Whoever does something
for a reason, then the reason’s existence must be prior than its
nonexistence for him. For if it is not prior, then it is not a reason in
itself. For whomever doing something is better than not doing it,
with the priority here indicated being part of his perfection—if he
does not do it, he will not obtain the better thing, and thus he will
not obtain ese ree ae his1 DES on his

```

49 The Book of Radiance
reason, but rather His action itself is pure good.
[52] It is not possible that the Necessary Being create things by
will. This is because when a thing brings something else into existence
by will in such a way that it not be enhanced [through temporal
change], then it will not be obtained by and it would not have had
the “will” prior to the act of willing. This is not the same as when
the multitude aver that the quality of “will” is such that being is
specified from nonbeing and from the mode possibility. [Such beliefs
are incorrect] because, given that all things must be equal before
the one who bestows his will [to his Master], there will be no
difference between doing something and not doing it. For whichever
mode is chosen then it has to be qualified 2as something specifically
```
determined {while there is no specificity iin causation]. Therefore,
```
we conclude that the Necessary Being, as well as the principle of
noncorporal entities, act neither by will nor for a specified objective
reason. Neither is aotice catenin existence by nature, because
```
whatever acts by nature is not a cognizant being; while, on the
```
contrary, we have demonstrated that the Necessary Being is a
knowing subject, and so are the [separate] Intellects. Therefore,
```
their acts are not by nature; only bodies act by nature.
```

51 The Book of Radiance
[55] The intention of whatever moves by volition is ۶ the same
as the motion itself. This is because motion is defined as “seeking
something,” But the celestial spheres do not seek anything lustfully
or out of anger. This is because in the spheres, there are no additional
parts pertaining to growth nor things that cause disruption nor
anything requiring linear motion. Nor do they have things
[additional] that impede their motion in space, nor things that
may destroy them on their paths. Therefore, the spheres have neither
lust nor ‘fury [attributes that cause motion ininanimals]. The motions
ofthe spheres, furthermore, is not for the sake of anything obtained
```
instantaneously. Otherwise, the spheres would cease to move; for if
```
something is potentially existent or not, it would impede them in
any case—which is not the case, since the spheres move continually
```
and not for the benefit of a lower entity; because a lower entity is
```
less worthy before it, for it moves continually for its sake.
It has been هر tiin the sciences that the smallest star in
the sphere of the fixed stars observed by humans is many times
larger than the Earth, and that the sun is more than 160 times
larger than the Earth. Imagine how enormous is the sphere that
contains the sun—and the spheres beyond it, which are even larger!
It has also been proven that the spheres cause the generation of
```
motion; it is therefore clear that the spheres do not reach perfection
```
by means of their effects. It is also clear that the spheres do not
move due to mutual love and the harmony of their bodies and
souls. For if it were so, the motions of all the spheres would be in
accordance with just one orbit, which is not observed to be the
case. Rather, the directions of their motions and their speeds are

different. Also, that the multitudes claim “a smaller thing moves

53 The Book of Radiance
[57] Also, if celestial matter remains in the same position, a potential
future position will never become actualized. And if future possibility
never enter a being, that being will be imperfect. Then observe that
the Intellects are in an actualized state [from every perspective],
and the spheres do not have potentiality in regard to their position.
However, all potential positions together cannot become actualized
at once. Thus, the intellect causes their positions one after another
```
in order; this is because no one can behold [or stop from moving]
```
simply the individuals of something but will learnof the species
one by one, and in order, from theindividuals.
[58] The object, or the “beloved,” of all of the celestial spheres is
not the same, single intellect. Otherwise, all celestial motion would
be the same, which is not the case. Further, what some have said,
that “the beloved of all celestial spheres is the same, single intellect,
and differences in the directions of motions have been so chosen
by it for the benefit of the lowest, for whom all directions are the
same” is wrong. Such a statement, were it justified in that theintellect
choose the direction-of motion to benefit the lowest, then it would
be eequally justified that it move for the benefit of the lowest or that
it move without direction. But the lowest does not have the rank
that would necessitate motion of the intellect in this direction instead
of another. Rather, just as all spheres share circular motion [although
different in orbital dimension and direction], they all have the same,
one “beloved,” which is the Necessary Being. But, inasmuch as
their motion does differ [in orbital attributes], each has a specific
beloved who is in the realm of the intellect, through whose
intermediation it receives the light of the Necessary Being. And,
since there are multiple spheres with multiple motions, thus the

[59] Now that you have learned that “body” is not obtained from
the Necessary Being, then what is obtained from it first is “intellect,”
just as we have said. Now, it would not be proper if only body were
to be obtained from this intellect, because then the universe would
be limited to that one body. But body cannot be the cause of bodies,
because the thing included in a set cannot be itself the cause of the
[same] set. This is because it is not possible that something cause
another thing greater and more noble than itself. Furthermore, the
thing included cannot itself be the cause of others also included [in
the set], because this would require the included to be caused after
the including. Nor that the necessity of the including thing’s existence
be the possibility of the existence of the included and the possibility
of existence be equal to the possibility of its nonexistence, which is
absurd. Even more absurd is this: from the possibility of the
nonexistence of the included, the necessity of the existence of void
would follow. But we have proven the impossibility of void in essence.

On the Causes of Generation ,the Good and Bad;
```
and on Predestination and Fate
[62] Know that it is not possible that the cause of anything generated
exists together with it totally [i.e., undifferentiated]. For if this were
so, then the effect would always exist together with the cause [at
the same time], which is not the case. Therefore, an ‘effect must
have a cause that is itself generated as a whole or as parts. This
same principle applies to a generated cause, or parts of it, which
itself needs a cause for its own generation. This applies to all things
generated which are causes, or parts of them, and never ceases to
be valid. Otherwise, for every generated entity there must be
unlimited causes. But limitless numbers cannot come together all
at once, because we have proven elsewhere that any ordered sequence
[i.e., that has an nth terms] of numbers whose individuals are
countable must have an upperbound. Therefore, the members of
the sequence of the causes of generated entities cannot all come
together at one time. And hence the observed [sequence of] causes
must necessarily be continually renewed and never end at a point
specified “first member.” Because whatever may be termed “first”
[at some given time] may later require a cause for its own generation,
leading to a circular sequence [without upper and lower bounds],
which isabsurd.
exist is “motion.” ’ Every motion may |cease,hh the exception of
the circular motion of celestial spheres. Also, these [observed]
generated entities are not specified by a place in the cosmos [i.e.,
```
are not coordinated in space]; therefore, their causes must be motions

61 The Book of Radiance
that the intellect Chane but the op change and are different
different, dissimilar effects due to the differences aamong thereceivers.
Some scholars have claimed that the generated entities cannot
continue indefinitely, that infinite generation is impossible because
if one thing is here and generated now, then all things are generated.
This is false, because one cannot make a judgment concerning “every
one” [member of a set] and apply it to “all” in all cases [at the same
time]. Because, for example, we can correctly say “every human fits
in a house,” but we cannot say “all humans fit in a house,” [if we
mean by “all” the sum total of individuals, together at the same
time]. Other scholars have claimed that when [at some time] each
and every one of all generated things become existent, then “all”
are existent, [which would lead to the impossibility of actual infinity].
```
But this is also a mistake because itconfuses “every” with “all”; the
```
```
latter taken as an actual, real collectivity; and , as we said, such
```
objections are unnecessary. Motions do not come together to form
```
a real, collective whole; rather, one continually becomes nonexistent
```
at a point, while another is generated at another point. Therefore
they do not have an “all” which is actual and real.
These same scholars have further claimed thus: Today is the last
```
day of all bygone days; therefore, there is an end to the sequence of
```
past days, and so there is also a beginning. This too is false, because
today is the last day not in the sense that it is the last after which no
```
other one will come; but it is a last after which there will be an
```
infinite [number] to come. Just as today is the first of “post- eternity”
```

[66] And that they say: We shall consider all past motions taken at
```

the supposition that all past ordered motions can form a set all at
the same time is impossible. Whatever is argued based on an
impossible supposition is itself absurd [i.e., all past events cannot
exist at one time to form an actual infinity]. The proof we previously
offered concerning finitude is correct when the set of numbers is
ordered. However, if there be order but not a set, such as celestial
```

[67] Predestination is God’s unique knowledge, Praised and Exalted_
is He. Divine providence is the detailed elaboration of primary
predestination and is the containment of individual, generated
```

```
and temporal extensions, as we shall see. Evil is not something
existent, but neither is the nonexistence of something nor the lack
of some [other] thing’s perfection. When a sixth finger iis taken as
an evil sign, it is because itit takes away the hand's beautiful form.
Because a thing that does not harm something else also does not
harm itself, it thus cannot be “evil.” Other things, such as snakes
```
and scorpions, are considered evil because they cause death; for if
```
they were not lethal they would not be called evil. Since the Necessary
Being is pure good, and Its essence is the most perfect of existent
entities <and of the intelligibles, then only pure good is generated
from It. And if there are real existent entities in which there is a
degree of evil, the evil is less than the good, which, nevertheless, has
caused the former’s existence. The ‘‘Pure Good” is the most noble
realm, which is the realm of Intellects and similar [separate,
noncorporal] things. As for water and fire, it is clear that their
benefit is greater than their harm, even though fire may at times
burn a dervish’s garment. To let go of abundant good to ward off a

(68) If someone should ask, “Since generated events are due to
```
predestination and divine will, then why are sinners punished?” the
reply would be that punishment is because of some external fault,
and it is not an act of vengeance out of anger. Rather, the soul itself
carries its own torment. Just as when someone is afflicted with a
disease because of a prior wrong. If someone should claim that
since it has been made clear that there is more good than evil, then
how is it that more people give in to lust, anger, and ignorance, and
are oblivious of the hereafter, and more are given to evil? [we respond
```
thus:] Know that the ranks of people in this world are three: the
```
```
highest are those possessing earthly happiness; the middle consisting
```
```
of many ranks itself; and the lowest, who are afflicted with financial
```
and bodily hardship. The former two ranks consist of more [people]
than the latter, while the middle rank alone comprises the greatest
number in the hereafter. There is more happiness and good than
```
evil and hardship; and happiness is not merely one kind.
```

in accordance with the individual’s capacity, and this has made

path to perfection. Now that you have learned that generated events
in this world come about because of the Dator Formarum, and it
does not change, then emanation gushes forth because of the
“capacity” [potentiality] of the receptacle. It is not possible that a
thing generated be without capacity or be a thing that will act in its
```

```
receptacle for [soul], but in۱ actuality it does receive it, because soul
is the body’s perfection, and its existence also depends on it.
Furthermore, should Prime Matter be generated, then its cause
would have become subject to change, and this is impossible. As we
said before, a separate entity is not subject to change with respect
to all modalities, otherwise its change will lead to change in the
Necessary Being, whichis provenan impossibility. Therefore, Primeیورو نوLa
Matter is eternal. And since it is not possible that all existent entities
existallat once—because bodies and corporeal things are finite, as
demonstrated by the necessity of finitude—and since the perfections
```
of possible things, their forms, souls and so on, are infinite; and
```
also since should these entities be assumed to be infinite alltogether
at once—while such is an impossible predication made of a possible
thing—they would remain infinite but as nonbeing. Therefore just
as the principles have infinite potentiality, created Prime Matter
also has infinite potentiality. Bodies are thus generated by the
Benevolence of God, Praised and Exalted be He, which forever
move directed towards celestial objectives. Then infinite capacities

are obtained from the Dator Formarum, as are infinite generated

```

<*> CHAPTER NINE
```
On the Immortality of the Soul;
```
Happiness and Insolence, and Similar Things
```
[73] Know that the soul is. immortal; it cannot be> conceived
```
```
annihilated. This is because its cause, the Active Intellect, iseternal;
```
therefore what is caused by it will be permanent, too. The soul is
not something naturally imprinted in a place, such that if the place
```
takes a different shape this would destroy it; then the capacity of
```
existence would be nullified in the soul, too. An example is the
heating of water: when it becomes very hot it is transformed into
air. And since the soul does not have a place, but is a substance by
which bodies are distinguished, then its nonexistence does not come
about by the annihilation of another bodily substance. In short,
what distinguishes life from death is the ceasing of attachment
[between substance and its body]. “Attachment” iis a relative added
accident, and the abolishing of relations [and added attributes]
does not necessitate the abolishing of substance. Thus, substance
continues as [long as] its cause continues. Although the body’s
potential is such that it requires the soul [in order to exist], the
soul is not aborted once the body is aborted. Take, for example, a
carpenter, who is only a distinguishing substance for the table [he
makes], then although he may die, that table still remains. The
Intellects never cease to exist, because they do not have a carrier,
nor are they [essentially] attached to a carrier other than their own
cause, which emanated them: the Necessary Being, which is eternal.
Therefore, the Realm of Intellect is eternal with It and depends
only on It. The separate entities never die. This is because their

70 The Book of Radiance
existence does not depend on an external cause, that they may be
corrupted and perish. Rather, the Intellects are self-conscious because
they are absolved of matter. Their self-consciousness is their life
```
and essential to them; therefore, they do not perish [i.e., become
```
nonexistent]. The master Plato has said the same thing concerning
the soul—that itbelies life toae And whatever is attributed as
Thus, the soul will never acceptanything that isو contrary.to its
```

```
[74] Know that a group from among the multitude think that
happiness and pleasure are nothing but copulation, eating, drinking,
and the like. Such people regard the angels and the.cherubim as
```
villainous; and they think that the state of the four-legged animals,
```
who possess the pleasures mentioned, are better than the angels
and that of the Heavenly beings.
[75] Know that “pleasure” is attaining perfection and good of
something and cognizance of it—when no obstacle bars the
cognizance. “Pain” isthe appearance of evil and malady by something
of which one iscognizant—when |no obstacle bars the cognizance.
It is possible that something pleasurable appear but no pleasure be
obtained, due to an obstacle—as when someone whose stomach is
```
diseased or full is repulsed by delicious food; but once the obstacle
```
is removed, the pleasure is attained. It is likewise possible that
something painful arrive without resulting iin pain due to an obstacle.

71 The Book of Radiance
Just as when someone who suffers from numbness will not feel
```
pain when struck; but when the numbness goes away, he will then
```
feel pain.
[76] There are pains and pleasures for each of the human faculties.
Sight feels pleasure by seeing things soft and is pained by seeing
things coarse. Smell finds pleasure by smelling things that have
good scent, and is pained by smelling foul odors. Hearing feels
pleasure by the sound of beautiful voices and is pained by course
sounds. The faculty of desire finds pleasure in the fulfillment of
```
passion; anger is fulfilled by revenge that causes pain to the person
```
with whom one is angry. The pleasure and pain of each faculty are
not known to the other faculties: for instance, smell does not find
pleasure in good-tasting food and is not pained by foul stuff. The
perfection. and pleasure of the soul is in receivingbeingfrom the
cause of causes of things and is also known 1 from theIntellects,
souls, celestial beings and the elemental—in that order. When the
soul, after. separation from the body, |is ‘imprinted with the
intelligibles, it then will partake of the intellectual realm and become
actualized as an intellect in every way.
[77] The perfection of the practical intellect is that it controls the
body and its acts—not its potentialities—and obtains the nature of
justice. Justice is virtue, bravery and, wisdom. Virtue provides soundee eet. peee

73 The Book of Radiance
of mental experience [to that of sensation]. That is, mental experience
of the Necessary Being and of the realm of intellect and souls, and
so on, [cannot be compared to] the experience of eating, for example,
and all other base experiences [of the senses]. Mental perception is
much stronger than senseperception, because the senses only
experience the external dimensions of things, while the mind
experiences the external as well as the inner [dimension] ofthings,
and more. This is because there are infinite objects for mental
cognition, while the senses only perceive a limited number of things.
Further, mental experience is more noble relative to what is the
```
“nearest” [entity] and is more necessary; because the senses do
```
perish, but the soul does not. Therefore, there are relative degrees
of experience in terms of the mind’s experience, as subject, of the
```
object and of experience per se in relation to senseperception; and
```
the pleasures of the mind are much [more noble] than those related
to sensation.
[80] If in this world we do not find pleasure from the Realm of
Intellects and do not become pained by experiencing ignorance
and base things, it is because we are intoxicated by the realm of
nature and unaware of ourr realm of‘of “self.”« But when the obstacles
[of the preoccupation with the senses] are removed, then the subject
who does possess a degree of perfection will experience infinite
pleasure as he beholds the Necessary Being, the High Heavens, and
the amazing things of the Realm of Lights. [Upon obtaining] such
an experience, the subject will forever remain with that experienced
```
pleasure: “Firmly established in favor of a Mighty Being.” Then
```
the subject will become a luminous intellect, will join the routes of

upon their hearts.”'” Of all of these three, they will receive pain
such that cannot be described, and “falling threefold” is an
indication of these three types of punishment. As for those who are

a while but will eventually find salvation.

sense in the Hereafter shall be more noble and better than what
they find here, because what the subject is has no relation to what
is there. The Lawgiver said: “Most of those who dwell in Paradise
are left alone and are elevated to the threshholds of Heaven.” And

0 This is an indication that thewhence those brought God drink.
drink of the holy is of Tasnim, which is a spring from which those
brought near God do drink, from which the souls of the happy are
nourished. Every soul that is separated from the body will obtain
```
its allotment from the Realm of Power; and from it, light will shine
```
upon the separate souls who will find pleasure therein. And infinite
lights will shine upon it, just as when mirrors are brought to face
each other. The evil ones will find pain in darkness and in each
other’s company. The one before will find pain from the one after,
and viceversa.
“ [84] Now that it has been made known that true pleasure lies in
| obtaining perfection and perceiving it, [you should know further]
that nothing is more complete than the Necessary Being, who is the
Greatest Knower, as well as the Known. Experiencing It is the greatest
of all. Thus, the pleasure of experiencing It is the greatest pleasure
of all and cannot be measured against anything else. “Love” is passion
```
for conceiving an essence’s presence; “Desire” is the soul’s movement
```
toward completing that passion. The desiring subject has partial
experience that, when completed, will cause the desire to end.
Therefore, the Necessary Being loves only Its own essence,and is
the object of Its own love as well as the objectofthe love ofothers.
Next in rank is the pleasure [experienced] by Intellects who, however,
have no “desire,” because they are free of potentiality and are
```
permanently actualized; next in rank is the love and pleasure of
```
souls themselves according to rank.

78 The Book of Radiance
```
(85) Know that metempsychosis is impossible, as agreed by all of
```
the Peripatetic philosophers. This is because when the body’s natural
```
disposition is complete, it then requires a soul; and should another
```
soul belonging to a different animal be obtained by it at this time,
then the result would be that a single animal possess two souls. But
every subject experiences only one soul in its self and does not
know itself more than as a single essence. Also, it is not necessary
that the time of one entity’s generation coincide with the time of
the corruption of another so that the generated and corrupted entities
correspond in number. Metempsychosis is the worst of beliefs and
is absolute nonsense.

- CHAPTER TEN “%
On Prophecy, Miracles, Miraculous Powers, Dreams,
and Similar Phenomena
]86[ Know that since each and every human cannot accomplish all
```
of their important affairs; and people are necessarily in need of
```
```
trade, retaliation, and marriage; and some people cannot avoid
```
[contact] with others, thus there must exist an obeyed |law and
codified canons. Therefore, a‘Lawgiver_ is necessary in every time
for every nation—a Lawgiver who would be virtuous, aware of
truths, and aided by the divine ‘realm of light.and power. Such a
Lawgiver must be capable of performing acts others are incapable
```
of; otherwise, they would not heed his words and would molest
```
and thwart him. The [extraordinary] deeds of the Lawgiver would
signify the truthfulness of his words and indicate that he is a legitimate
messenger for humankind. The Lawgiver would instigate people to
do good deeds and would encourage them to pray to God. He
would render prayer necessary for people so they would not forget
their duties, and their [beliefs] would become firm.
[87] There are several conditions for the prophet [Lawgiver]. He
must be appointed apostle by the High Heavens, which is a condition
specific to prophets. The rest of the conditions, such as extraordinary
acts, foretelling visions, or learning truths without a teacher, may
be possessed by other great divinely informed souls. Also, itispossible
that some saints and lofty humans be divinely informed. It is not
necessary that each and every prophet possess truths of the highest
nature [to an extent more than anyone else], because many of the
learned people and scholars of their [Muslim] nation—such as Abu
Bakr, ‘Umar, “Uthman, ‘Ali, Hudhayfa, Hasan al-Basri, Dhu’l-Nun

(88] It is not strange that great prophets—such as Enoch, Abraham
```
```
and our Lawgiver (God’s praise be upon them all) and others—have
```
learned truths without a human teacher, because people differ in
their degree of intuition. There may be someone who does not
benefit at all even from the highest degree of intelligence, due to
excessive stolidity. There may be, on the other hand, people who
_are capable of intuiting many problems without a human teacher.
Therefore, it is not farfetched that there exist some people with
powerful intuitions who may learn much of the sciences in but a
short period of time. Nor is it strange that extraordinary acts—such
as producing earthquakes and eclipses, curing disease, and taming
wild beasts and birds—be performed by great prophets. You have
learned that the body is under the soul’s control, and that separate
entities control the world of matter. Have you not observed how
```
the body heats up from the soul’s anger? Fancy, too, has effects;
```
there are people who, out of fancy throw themselves down from
high places. Therefore, if such as I have specified is the state of
things I have cited, then it is not strange that a person whose soul is
enlightened by the Light of Truth and the High Heavens—which

81 The Book of Radiance
light is the elixir of knowledge and power—may control the realm
of matter, and his words and prayers will be heard due to his soul’s
enlightenment. If you want to know the cause of foretelling visions,
a law must be established.
[89] Know that the souls of the celestial spheres are cognizant of
their own motions and of their own effects. They possess universal
knowledge, know arpa Me amcenitel ie know every form and
effect that is in this world. When a celestial sphere arrives at a
specific point [in its orbit] it becomes cognizant of whatever comes
about as a result of achieving that point. In short, it comprehends
all temporal causes of events from past to future, as well as the
present. Our souls are not disjointed from the ‘spheres, but
attachment to body prevents us from conjunction with them.
```
(90) While sleeping, the external senses are not acting, but the
```
internal senses—especially the faculty of imagination—preoccupy
the soul. Therefore, when bodily attachments are lessened, the soul
joins the celestial souls—as, [for example], during some people’s
dreams or the experiences of epileptics or choleric people. The
same is experienced by prophets and some saints who perform
meditation and purify their natures, and other [similar acts], and
thus their souls become related to the souls of the celestial spheres.
The same is true of some diviners who have innate weakness [in
terms of bodily preoccupations]. It may also be that they seek
assistance from things that weaken the outer and inner senses—as
when children are busied with things that mesmerize their eyes,
